alt="Flag of the United Kingdom.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/32px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png" decoding="async" width="32" height="16" href="/wiki/Outline_of_political_science#Politics_by_region" title="Outline of political science">Other countries</a></li></ul>\n</div></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r992953826">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}.mw-parser-output .navbox .navbar{display:block;font-size:100%}.mw-parser-output .navbox-title .navbar{float:left;text-align:left;margin-right:0.5em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Politics_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Template:Politics of the United Kingdom"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Politics_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Template talk:Politics of the United Kingdom"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Politics_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>pound sterling</b> (symbol: <b><a href="/wiki/Pound_sign" title="Pound sign">\xc2\xa3</a></b>; <a href="/wiki/ISO_4217" title="ISO 4217">ISO code</a>: <b>GBP</b>), known in some contexts simply as <b>the pound</b> or  <b>sterling</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> is the official <a href="/wiki/Currency" title="Currency">currency</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jersey" title="Jersey">Jersey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guernsey" title="Guernsey">Guernsey</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gibraltar" title="Gibraltar">Gibraltar</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Georgia_and_the_South_Sandwich_Islands" title="South Georgia and the South Sandwich Islands">South Georgia and the South Sandwich Islands</a>, the <a href="/wiki/British_Antarctic_Territory" title="British Antarctic Territory">British Antarctic Territory</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Tristan_da_Cunha" title="Tristan da Cunha">Tristan da Cunha</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> It is subdivided into 100 <i>pence</i> (singular: <i>penny</i>, abbreviated: <i>p</i>). The "pound sterling" is the oldest currency in continuous use.  Some nations that do not use sterling also have currencies called the <i><a href="/wiki/Pound_(currency)" title="Pound (currency)">pound</a></i>.\n</p><p>Sterling is the fourth most-traded currency in the <a href="/wiki/Foreign_exchange_market" title="Foreign exchange market">foreign exchange market</a>, after the <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">United States dollar</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">euro</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_yen" title="Japanese yen">Japanese yen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Together with those three currencies and the <a href="/wiki/Renminbi" title="Renminbi">Chinese yuan</a>, it forms the <a href="/wiki/Basket_of_currencies" class="mw-redirect" title="Basket of currencies">basket of currencies</a> which <a href="/wiki/Special_drawing_rights#Value_definition" title="Special drawing rights">calculate the value</a> of <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">IMF</a> <a href="/wiki/Special_drawing_rights" title="Special drawing rights">special drawing rights</a>. As of 30&#160;September&#160;2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, sterling is also the fourth most-held <a href="/wiki/Reserve_currency" title="Reserve currency">reserve currency</a> in <a href="/wiki/Foreign_exchange_reserves" title="Foreign exchange reserves">global reserves</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IMFCOFER_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IMFCOFER-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The British <a href="/wiki/Crown_dependencies" class="mw-redirect" title="Crown dependencies">Crown dependencies</a> of Guernsey, Jersey and the Isle of Man produce their own local issues of sterling (the <a href="/wiki/Guernsey_pound" title="Guernsey pound">Guernsey pound</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Jersey_pound" title="Jersey pound">Jersey pound</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Manx_pound" title="Manx pound">Manx pound</a>) which are considered fully equivalent to UK sterling in their respective regions.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The pound sterling is also used in the <a href="/wiki/British_Overseas_Territories" title="British Overseas Territories">British Overseas Territories</a> <a href="/wiki/Gibraltar" title="Gibraltar">Gibraltar</a> (alongside the <a href="/wiki/Gibraltar_pound" title="Gibraltar pound">Gibraltar pound</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Falkland_Islands" title="Falkland Islands">Falkland Islands</a> (alongside the <a href="/wiki/Falkland_Islands_pound" title="Falkland Islands pound">Falkland Islands pound</a>), and in <a href="/wiki/Saint_Helena,_Ascension_and_Tristan_da_Cunha" title="Saint Helena, Ascension and Tristan da Cunha">Saint Helena, Ascension and Tristan da Cunha</a> (alongside the <a href="/wiki/Saint_Helena_pound" title="Saint Helena pound">Saint Helena pound</a>), others having transitioned to dollar currencies, such as <a href="/wiki/Bermuda" title="Bermuda">Bermuda</a> in 1970. The <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">Bank of England</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Central_bank" title="Central bank">central bank</a> for the pound sterling, issuing its own banknotes, and regulating issuance of banknotes by private banks in Scotland and Northern Ireland. Sterling banknotes issued by other jurisdictions are not regulated by the Bank of England; their governments <a href="/wiki/Currency_board" title="Currency board">guarantee convertibility at par</a>. The pound sterling was also used to varying degrees by colonies in the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Names"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Names</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-2"><a href="#Etymology"><span class="tocnumber">1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Etymology</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#Symbol"><span class="tocnumber">1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Symbol</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a href="#Currency_code"><span class="tocnumber">1.3</span> <span class="toctext">Currency code</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-5"><a href="#Cable"><span class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#1601-1816"><span class="tocnumber">3.7</span> <span class="toctext">1601-1816</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a (1801)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Use_in_the_Empire"><span class="tocnumber">3.10</span> <span class="toctext">Use in the Empire</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Gold_standard"><span class="tocnumber">3.11</span> <span class="toctext">Gold standard</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a crisis</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#1979\xe2\x80\x931989"><span class="tocnumber">3.16</span> <span class="toctext">1979\xe2\x80\x931989</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-27"><a href="#Following_the_Deutsche_Mark"><span class="tocnumber">3.17</span> <span class="toctext">Following the Deutsche Mark</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 <span class="toctext">Recent exchange rates</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-32"><a href="#Annual_inflation_rate"><span class="tocnumber">3.22</span> <span class="toctext">Annual inflation rate</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#Coins"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Coins</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-34"><a href="#Pre-decimal_coins"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Pre-decimal coins</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-35"><a href="#Decimal_coins"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Decimal coins</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#Banknotes"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Banknotes</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-37"><a href="#Polymer_banknotes"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Polymer banknotes</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-38"><a href="#Monetary_policy"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Monetary policy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-39"><a href="#Legal_tender_and_national_issues"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Legal tender and national issues</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-40"><a href="#Value"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Value</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-41"><a href="#Exchange_rate"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Exchange rate</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-42"><a href="#Reserve"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Reserve</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-43"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-44"><a href="#Footnotes"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Footnotes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-45"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-46"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-47"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Names">Names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Names">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The full official name <i>pound sterling</i> (<a href="/wiki/English_plural#Plurals_of_compound_nouns" class="mw-redirect" title="English plural">plural</a>: <i>pounds sterling</i>), is used mainly in formal contexts and also when it is necessary to distinguish the United Kingdom currency from <a href="/wiki/Pound_(currency)" title="Pound (currency)">other currencies with the same name</a>. Otherwise the term <i>pound</i> is normally used. The currency name is sometimes abbreviated to just <i>sterling</i>, particularly in the wholesale financial markets, but not when referring to specific amounts; for example, "Payment is accepted in sterling" but never "These cost five sterling". The abbreviations "ster." and "stg." are sometimes used. The term "British pound" is sometimes used in less formal contexts, but it is not an official name of the currency.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sterling_silver#Etymology" title="Sterling silver">Sterling silver \xc2\xa7&#160;Etymology</a></div>\n<p>There are various theories regarding the origin of the term "pound sterling". The Oxford English Dictionary states that the "most plausible" etymology is derivation from the Old English <i>steorra</i> for "star" with the added <a href="/wiki/Diminutive" title="Diminutive">diminutive</a> suffix "-ling", to mean "little star" and to refer to a <a href="/wiki/Silver_penny" class="mw-redirect" title="Silver penny">silver penny</a> of the English Normans.<sup id="cite_ref-Entry_189985_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Entry_189985-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Another argument that the <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a> was the origin for both the origin of its definition and manufacture, and in its name is that the German name for the Baltic is "Ostsee", or "East Sea", and from this the Baltic merchants were called "Osterlings", or "Easterlings".<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> In 1260, Henry III granted them a charter of protection and land for their Kontor, the <a href="/wiki/Steelyard" title="Steelyard">Steelyard of London</a>, which by the 1340s was also called "Easterlings Hall", or Esterlingeshalle.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Because the League\'s money was not frequently debased like that of England, English traders stipulated to be paid in pounds of the "Easterlings", which was contracted to "\'sterling".<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Encyclopedia_Britannica" class="mw-redirect" title="Encyclopedia Britannica">Encyclopedia Britannica</a> states the (pre-Norman) Anglo-Saxon kingdoms had silver coins called \'sterlings\' and that the compound noun \'pound sterling\' was derived from a pound (weight) of these sterlings.<sup id="cite_ref-britannica1_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britannica1-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbol">Symbol</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Symbol">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pound_sign" title="Pound sign">pound sign</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Currency_sign" class="mw-redirect" title="Currency sign">currency sign</a> for the pound is <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886049734">.mw-parser-output .monospaced{font-family:monospace,monospace}</style><span class="monospaced">\xc2\xa3</span>, which is usually written with a single cross-bar (as on modern <a href="/wiki/Banknote" title="Banknote">banknotes</a> exclusively since 1975).<sup id="cite_ref-BoEoldnotes_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BoEoldnotes-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> A variation with a double cross-bar (<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734"/><span class="monospaced">\xe2\x82\xa4</span>) has been used intermittently with <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734"/><span class="monospaced">\xc2\xa3</span> since the earliest banknotes of 1725 when both were used.<sup id="cite_ref-BoEoldnotes_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BoEoldnotes-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Historically, a simple capital L was used in newspapers, books and letters.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>  The symbol derives from medieval Latin documents: the <a href="/wiki/Blackletter" title="Blackletter">black-letter</a> "L" (<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\mathfrak {L}}}">\n  <semantics>\n    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n            <mi mathvariant="fraktur">L</mi>\n          </mrow>\n        </mrow>\n      </mstyle>\n    </mrow>\n    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\mathfrak {L}}}</annotation>\n  </semantics>\n</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f1cc2d02222bcba1e741979a145f0317df3cda81" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.548ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\\mathfrak  {L}}"/></span>) was the abbreviation for <i><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Roman_units_of_measurement#Weight" title="Ancient Roman units of measurement">libra</a></i>, the basic Roman unit of weight, which became an <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">English</a> unit of weight defined as the <a href="/wiki/Pound_(weight)#Tower_pound" class="mw-redirect" title="Pound (weight)">tower pound</a> of <a href="/wiki/Sterling_silver" title="Sterling silver">sterling silver</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ViewOfCoinage_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ViewOfCoinage-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> In the British pre-decimal (<a href="/wiki/Duodecimal" title="Duodecimal">duodecimal</a>) currency system, the term <a href="/wiki/%C2%A3sd" title="\xc2\xa3sd">\xc2\xa3sd</a> (or Lsd) for pounds, <a href="/wiki/Shilling" title="Shilling">shillings</a> and <a href="/wiki/Penny" title="Penny">pence</a> referred to the Roman words <i>libra</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Solidus_(coin)" title="Solidus (coin)">solidus</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Denarius" title="Denarius">denarius</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-britannica1_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britannica1-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Currency_code">Currency code</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Currency code">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/ISO_4217" title="ISO 4217">ISO 4217 currency code</a> is <b>GBP</b>, formed from "GB", the <a href="/wiki/ISO_3166-1_alpha-2" title="ISO 3166-1 alpha-2">ISO 3166-1 alpha-2</a> code for the United Kingdom, and the first letter of "pound". Occasionally, the abbreviation "UKP" is used but this is non-standard because the <a href="/wiki/ISO_3166" title="ISO 3166">ISO 3166</a> country code for the United Kingdom is GB (see <a href="/wiki/Terminology_of_the_British_Isles#Terminology_in_detail" title="Terminology of the British Isles">Terminology of the British Isles</a>). The <a href="/wiki/Crown_dependency" class="mw-redirect" title="Crown dependency">Crown dependencies</a> use their own (non-ISO) codes: GGP (<a href="/wiki/Guernsey_pound" title="Guernsey pound">Guernsey pound</a>), JEP (<a href="/wiki/Jersey_pound" title="Jersey pound">Jersey pound</a>) and IMP (<a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man_pound" class="mw-redirect" title="Isle of Man pound">Isle of Man pound</a>). Stock prices are often quoted in pence, so traders may refer to <a href="/wiki/Pence_sterling" class="mw-redirect" title="Pence sterling">pence sterling</a>, GBX (sometimes GBp), when listing stock prices.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cable">Cable</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Cable">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cable_(foreign_exchange)" title="Cable (foreign exchange)">Cable (foreign exchange)</a></div>\n<p>The exchange rate of the pound sterling against the <a href="/wiki/US_dollar" class="mw-redirect" title="US dollar">US dollar</a> is referred to as "cable" in the wholesale <a href="/wiki/Foreign_exchange_market" title="Foreign exchange market">foreign exchange markets</a>. The origins of this term are attributed to the fact that in the 1800s, the GBP/USD exchange rate was transmitted via transatlantic cable. Forex traders of GBP/USD are sometimes referred to as "cable dealers".<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> JPY/USD is the other currency pair with its own name, known as "fiber".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Quid_.28slang.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Quid_(slang)">Quid (slang)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Quid (slang)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>A common slang term for the pound sterling or pound is <i>quid</i>, which is singular and plural, except in the common phrase "quids in!".<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The term may have come via Italian immigrants from "<a href="/wiki/Italian_scudo" title="Italian scudo">scudo</a>", the name for a number of coins used in Italy until the 19th century; or from Latin \'quid\' via the common phrase <i><a href="/wiki/Quid_pro_quo" title="Quid pro quo">quid pro quo</a></i>, literally, "what for what", or, figuratively, "An equal exchange or substitution".<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Subdivisions_and_other_units">Subdivisions and other units</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Subdivisions and other units">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decimal_coinage">Decimal coinage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Decimal coinage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Since <a href="/wiki/Decimalisation" title="Decimalisation">decimalisation</a> on <a href="/wiki/Decimal_Day" title="Decimal Day">Decimal Day</a> in 1971, the pound has been divided into 100 pence (denoted on coinage, until 1981, as "new pence"). The symbol for the penny is "p"; hence an amount such as 50p (\xc2\xa30.50) properly pronounced "fifty pence" is often pronounced "fifty pee" /f\xc9\xaafti pi/. This also helped to distinguish between new and old pence amounts during the changeover to the decimal system. A decimal <a href="/wiki/Halfpenny_(British_decimal_coin)" title="Halfpenny (British decimal coin)">halfpenny</a> was issued until 1984 but was removed due to having a higher cost to manufacture than its face value.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-decimal">Pre-decimal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Pre-decimal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="infobox"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-above" style="background-color: lightpink; padding: 0.5em 0em;"><a href="/wiki/%C2%A3sd" title="\xc2\xa3sd">Pre-decimal</a> pound</th></tr><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-header" style="background-color: lightpink;">Denominations</th></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Subunit</th><td class="infobox-data"></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047488"/><span class="den">20</span></span></span></th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Shilling_(British_pre-decimal_coin)" class="mw-redirect" title="Shilling (British pre-decimal coin)">shilling</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047488"/><span class="nobold">&#8194;<link the most frequently used ones.</i></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label"><a href="/wiki/Plural" title="Plural">Plural</a></th><td class="infobox-data">Pounds</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" coin)">penny</a></span></th><td class="infobox-data">pence</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label"><a href="/wiki/Currency_symbol" title="Currency symbol">Symbol</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Pound_sign" title="Pound sign">\xc2\xa3</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047488"/><span class="nobold">&#8194;<a href="/wiki/Shilling_(British_pre-decimal_coin)" class="mw-redirect" title="Shilling (British pre-decimal coin)">shilling</a></span></th><td class="infobox-data">s</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047488"/><span class="nobold">&#8194;<a href="/wiki/Penny_(British_pre-decimal_coin)" title="Penny (British pre-decimal coin)">penny</a></span></th><td class="infobox-data">d</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label"><a href="/wiki/Banknotes_of_the_pound_sterling" title="Banknotes of the pound sterling">Banknotes</a></th><td class="infobox-data"></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047488"/><span class="nobold">&#8194;Freq. used</span></th><td class="infobox-data"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r979066050"/><ul class="cslist"><li>5s</li><li>10s</li><li>\xc2\xa31</li><li>\xc2\xa35</li><li>\xc2\xa310</li><li>\xc2\xa320</li></ul></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047488"/><span class="nobold">&#8194;Rarely used</span></th><td class="infobox-data"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r979066050"/><ul class="cslist"><li>\xc2\xa350</li><li>\xc2\xa3100</li></ul></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label"><a href="/wiki/Coins_of_the_pound_sterling" title="Coins of the pound sterling">Coins</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r979066050"/><ul class="cslist"><li>1d</li><li>3d</li><li>6d</li><li>1s</li><li>2s</li><li>2s6d</li><li>5s</li></ul></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-header" style="background-color: lightpink;">Demographics</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-below"><div style="font-size:85%; text-align:left;">This infobox shows the latest status before this currency was rendered obsolete.</div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/%C2%A3sd" title="\xc2\xa3sd">\xc2\xa3sd</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:De_Alice%27s_Abenteuer_im_Wunderland_Carroll_pic_25.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/De_Alice%27s_Abenteuer_im_Wunderland_Carroll_pic_25.jpg/220px-De_Alice%27s_Abenteuer_im_Wunderland_Carroll_pic_25.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/De_Alice%27s_Abenteuer_im_Wunderland_Carroll_pic_25.jpg/330px-De_Alice%27s_Abenteuer_im_Wunderland_Carroll_pic_25.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/De_Alice%27s_Abenteuer_im_Wunderland_Carroll_pic_25.jpg/440px-De_Alice%27s_Abenteuer_im_Wunderland_Carroll_pic_25.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2646" data-file-height="2046" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:De_Alice%27s_Abenteuer_im_Wunderland_Carroll_pic_25.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/The_Hatter" class="mw-redirect" title="The Hatter">The Hatter</a>\'s hat shows an example of the old pre-decimal system: the hat costs half a guinea (10 shillings and 6 pence).</div></div></div>\n<p>Before <a href="/wiki/%C2%A3sd#Decimalisations" title="\xc2\xa3sd">decimalisation in 1971</a>, the pound was divided into 20 <a href="/wiki/British_shilling_coin" class="mw-redirect" title="British shilling coin">shillings</a> and each shilling into 12 <a href="/wiki/Old_penny_sterling" class="mw-redirect" title="Old penny sterling">pence</a>, making 240 pence to the pound. The symbol for the shilling was "<i>s</i>."\xe2\x80\x94not from the first letter of "shilling", but from the Latin <i><a href="/wiki/Solidus_(coin)" title="Solidus (coin)">solidus</a></i>. The symbol for the penny was "<i>d</i>.", from the French <i>denier</i>, from the Latin <i><a href="/wiki/Denarius" title="Denarius">denarius</a></i> (the solidus and denarius were Roman coins). A mixed sum of shillings and pence, such as 3 shillings and 6 pence, was written as "3/6" or "3<i>s</i>. 6<i>d</i>." and spoken as "three and six" or "three and sixpence" except for "1/1," "2/1" etc., which were spoken as "one and a penny", "two and a penny", etc. 5 shillings, for example, was written as "5<i>s</i>." or, more commonly, "5/\xe2\x80\x93".\nVarious coin denominations had, and in some cases continue to have, special names\xe2\x80\x94such as <a href="/wiki/Florin_(British_coin)" title="Florin (British coin)">florin</a> (2s), <a href="/wiki/Crown_(British_coin)" title="Crown (British coin)">crown</a> (5s), <a href="/wiki/Half_crown_(British_coin)" title="Half crown (British coin)">half crown</a> (2s6d). <a href="/wiki/Farthing_(British_coin)" title="Farthing (British coin)">farthing</a> (<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016"/><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">4</span></span>d), <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_(British_coin)" title="Sovereign (British coin)">sovereign</a> (\xc2\xa31) and <a href="/wiki/Guinea_(British_coin)" class="mw-redirect" title="Guinea (British coin)">guinea</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Q.v." class="mw-redirect" title="Q.v.">q.v.</a></i>). See <a href="/wiki/Coins_of_the_pound_sterling" title="Coins of the pound sterling">Coins of the pound sterling</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_British_banknotes_and_coins" title="List of British banknotes and coins">List of British coins and banknotes</a> for details.\n</p><p>By the 1950s, coins of Kings <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="George III of the United Kingdom">George III</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="George IV of the United Kingdom">George IV</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="William IV of the United Kingdom">William IV</a> had disappeared from circulation, but coins (at least the penny) bearing the head of every British king or queen from <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a> onwards could be found in circulation. Silver coins were replaced by those in <a href="/wiki/Cupro-nickel" class="mw-redirect" title="Cupro-nickel">cupro-nickel</a> in 1947, and by the 1960s the silver coins were rarely seen. Silver/cupro-nickel shillings (from any period after 1816) and <a href="/wiki/Two_shilling_coin" class="mw-redirect" title="Two shilling coin">florins</a> (2 shillings) remained legal tender after decimalisation (as 5p and 10p respectively) until 1990 and 1993 respectively, but are now officially demonetised.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The pound sterling emerged after the adoption of <a href="/wiki/French_livre" title="French livre">Carolingian monetary system</a> in England c 800 CE. Here is a summary of changes to its value in terms of silver or gold until 1914. \n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<caption>Value of \xc2\xa31, grams\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Year</th>\n<th>g silver</th>\n<th>g gold\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="#Anglo-Saxon,_c_800_CE">800</a></td>\n<td>349.9</td>\n<td>-\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="#Medieval,_1158_CE">1158</a></td>\n<td>323.7</td>\n<td>-\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="#1351">1351</a></td>\n<td>258.9</td>\n<td>23.21\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="#1412">1412</a></td>\n<td>215.8</td>\n<td>20.89\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="#1464">1464</a></td>\n<td>172.6</td>\n<td>15.47\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="#Tudor,_1551">1551</a></td>\n<td>115.1</td>\n<td>10.31\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="#1601-1816">1601</a></td>\n<td>111.4</td>\n<td>variable\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1717</td>\n<td>111.4</td>\n<td>7.32238\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="#Gold_standard">1816</a></td>\n<td>-</td>\n<td>7.32238\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Since the suspension of the gold standard in 1931 the pound sterling has been <a href="/wiki/Fiat_money" title="Fiat money">fiat money</a>, with its value determined by its continued acceptance in the national and international economy. The pound sterling is the world\'s oldest currency still in use and which has been in continuous use since its inception.<sup id="cite_ref-Rendall_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rendall-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Anglo-Saxon.2C_c_800_CE"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Anglo-Saxon,_c_800_CE">Anglo-Saxon, c 800 CE</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Anglo-Saxon, c 800 CE">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_pound" title="Anglo-Saxon pound">Anglo-Saxon pound</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Pound.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/The_Pound.jpg/220px-The_Pound.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/The_Pound.jpg/330px-The_Pound.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/The_Pound.jpg/440px-The_Pound.jpg 2x" data-file-width="908" data-file-height="681" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Pound.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A pound = 20 <a href="/wiki/Shilling" title="Shilling">shillings</a> = 240 <a href="/wiki/Silver_penny" class="mw-redirect" title="Silver penny">silver pennies</a> (formerly)</div></div></div>\n<p>The <i>pound</i> was a unit of account in <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxon</a> England, equal to 240 silver <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_English_penny_(c._600-1066)" class="mw-redirect" title="History of the English penny (c. 600-1066)">pence</a> (the plural of penny) and equivalent to one <a href="/wiki/Pound_(mass)" title="Pound (mass)">pound weight</a> of silver. It evolved into the modern British <a href="/wiki/Currency" title="Currency">currency</a>, the pound sterling.\n</p><p>The accounting system of four <a href="/wiki/Farthing_(British_coin)" title="Farthing (British coin)">farthings</a> = one <a href="/wiki/Penny" title="Penny">penny</a>, twelve pence = one <a href="/wiki/Shilling" title="Shilling">shilling</a>, twenty shillings = one pound, was adopted from that introduced by <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Francia" title="Francia">Frankish Empire</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Livre_carolingienne" class="mw-redirect" title="Livre carolingienne">livre carolingienne</a>). The penny was abbreviated to  \'d\', from <i><a href="/wiki/Denarius" title="Denarius">denarius</a></i>, Latin for penny; \'s\' from <a href="/wiki/Solidus_(coin)" title="Solidus (coin)">solidus</a>, for shilling; and \'L\' (subsequently <a href="/wiki/Pound_symbol" class="mw-redirect" title="Pound symbol">\xc2\xa3</a>) from <i><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Roman_units_of_measurement#Weight" title="Ancient Roman units of measurement">Libra</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/French_livre" title="French livre">Livre</a></i> for the pound.\n</p><p>The origins of sterling lie in the reign of <a href="/wiki/Offa_of_Mercia" title="Offa of Mercia">King Offa of Mercia</a> (757\xe2\x80\x93796), who introduced the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_English_penny_(c._600-1066)" class="mw-redirect" title="History of the English penny (c. 600-1066)">silver penny</a>. It represented the <i>denarius</i> of the new currency system of <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Frankish_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Frankish Empire">Frankish Empire</a>. As in the Carolingian system, 240 pence  weighed one <a href="/wiki/Pound_(mass)" title="Pound (mass)">pound</a>, a unit corresponding to Charlemagne\'s <i>livre</i>, with the shilling  corresponding to Charlemagne\'s <i>solidus</i> and equal to twelve pence.\n</p><p>At the time of the penny\'s introduction, it weighed 22<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016"/><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">2</span></span> troy <a href="/wiki/Grain_(mass)" class="mw-redirect" title="Grain (mass)">grains</a> of fine silver (32 tower grains or 1.458 g), so the Mercian pound weighed 5,400 troy grains (the Mercian pound became the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Tower_pound" class="mw-redirect" title="Tower pound">tower pound</a>, which also weighed 5,400 troy grains, equivalent to 7,680 tower grains or 349.9g). While fractional <a href="/wiki/Halfpenny_(British_pre-decimal_coin)" title="Halfpenny (British pre-decimal coin)">halfpennies</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/Farthing_(English_coin)" title="Farthing (English coin)">farthings</a> worth <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016"/><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">4</span></span> penny were also minted, small change was more commonly produced by cutting up a whole penny.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Medieval.2C_1158_CE"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval,_1158_CE">Medieval, 1158 CE</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Medieval, 1158 CE">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:England_longcross_penny_Henry_III_Canterbury_mint.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/England_longcross_penny_Henry_III_Canterbury_mint.jpg/220px-England_longcross_penny_Henry_III_Canterbury_mint.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/England_longcross_penny_Henry_III_Canterbury_mint.jpg/330px-England_longcross_penny_Henry_III_Canterbury_mint.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/England_longcross_penny_Henry_III_Canterbury_mint.jpg/440px-England_longcross_penny_Henry_III_Canterbury_mint.jpg 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="450" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:England_longcross_penny_Henry_III_Canterbury_mint.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Penny of Henry III, 13th century</div></div></div>\n<p>The early pennies were struck from fine silver (as pure as was available). However, <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_English_penny_(1154%E2%80%931485)" title="History of the English penny (1154\xe2\x80\x931485)">in 1158, a new coinage</a> was introduced by King <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">Henry II</a> (known as the <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_English_penny_(1154%E2%80%931485)" title="History of the English penny (1154\xe2\x80\x931485)">Tealby penny</a></i>) which was struck from 92.5% silver; hence 1.349 g fine silver in a penny. This coinage standard, called <a href="/wiki/Sterling_silver" title="Sterling silver">sterling silver</a>, has been maintained until the 20th century. Sterling silver is harder than the 99.9% <a href="/wiki/Fine_silver" class="mw-redirect" title="Fine silver">fine silver</a> that was traditionally used and so sterling silver coins did not wear down as rapidly as fine silver coins.\n</p><p>The introduction in 1266 and subsequent popularity of the larger French <a href="/wiki/Groat_(coin)" title="Groat (coin)">gros tournois</a> coins led to additional denominations in the form of <a href="/wiki/Fourpence_(British_coin)" title="Fourpence (British coin)">groats</a> worth four pence &amp; <a href="/wiki/Twopence_(British_pre-decimal_coin)" title="Twopence (British pre-decimal coin)">half groats</a> worth two pence. A <a href="/wiki/Gold_penny" title="Gold penny">gold penny</a> weighing twice the silver penny and valued at 20 pence was also issued in 1257 but was not successful.\n</p><p>The English kings managed to keep the penny nearly unchanged for over 500 years until 1344, avoiding the frequent coin debasements &amp; the fragmentation of currency areas occurring in the rest of Continental Europe. The English groat denominated as 4 pence contrasts with the French gros tournois denominated 12 deniers (pence) and the Venetian grosso denominated 26 denari.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1351">1351</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: 1351">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward_III_noble.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Edward_III_noble.jpg/220px-Edward_III_noble.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="109" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Edward_III_noble.jpg/330px-Edward_III_noble.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Edward_III_noble.jpg/440px-Edward_III_noble.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="248" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward_III_noble.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Edward III noble (80 pence), 1354-55</div></div></div>\n<p>The introduction of gold coins received from <a href="/wiki/Flanders" title="Flanders">Flanders</a> as payment for English wool provided substantial economic &amp; trade opportunities but also unsettled the pound sterling for the next 200 years.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The first monetary changes in 1344 consisted of\n</p>\n<ul><li>English pennies reduced to 20<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016"/><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">4</span></span> grains of sterling silver (1.214 g fine silver), and</li>\n<li>Gold <a href="/wiki/Florin_(English_coin)" title="Florin (English coin)">double florins</a> weighing 108 grains (6.998 grams) and valued at 6 shillings (or 72 pence)</li></ul>\n<p>The resulting gold-silver ratio of 12.5 was much higher than the ratio of 11 prevailing in the Continent, draining England of its silver coinage &amp; requring a more permanent remedy in 1351 in the form of\n</p>\n<ul><li>Pennies reduced further to 18 grains of sterling silver (1.079 g fine silver), and</li>\n<li>New <a href="/wiki/Noble_(English_coin)" title="Noble (English coin)">Gold Noble</a> coins weighing 120 grains (7.776 g) of the finest gold possible at 191/192 or 99.48% fine, and valued at 6 shillings 8 pence (80 pence, or <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016"/><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">3</span></span>rd of a pound); hence 7.735 g fine gold in a Noble.</li></ul>\n<p>These gold nobles, together with half-nobles (40 pence) and farthings or quarter-nobles (20 pence) would form a widely-accepted medium of payment between England &amp; Europe.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1412">1412</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: 1412">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The exigencies of the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%E2%80%99_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Hundred Years\xe2\x80\x99 War">Hundred Years\xe2\x80\x99 War</a> during the reign of King Henry IV resulted in a further reduction in the English penny to 15 grains sterling silver (0.899 g fine silver) and the Half-Noble to 54 grains (3.481 g fine gold). The Gold-Silver Ratio reached its bottom of 10.\n</p><p>After the French monetary reform of 1421 the gold half-noble (<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016"/><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">6</span></span>th pound, 40 pence) was worth close to one <a href="/wiki/French_franc#History" title="French franc">Livre Parisis (French pound)</a> or 20 sols, while the silver half-groat (2 pence) was worth close to 1 sol parisis. Also, after the Flemish monetary reform of 1434, the new <a href="/wiki/Dutch_guilder#History" title="Dutch guilder">Flemish Guilder (pound)</a> was valued close to 40 pence while the <a href="/wiki/Stuiver" title="Stuiver">Flemish stuiver (shilling)</a> was valued close to 2 pence sterling. This approximate equivalence of English half-nobles &amp; half-groats to Continental livres &amp; sols persisted up to the 1560s and facilitated the issue of identical coin denominations on both sides of the channel.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1464">1464</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: 1464">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Bullion_Famine" title="Great Bullion Famine">Great Bullion Famine</a> of the mid-15th century resulted in another reduction in the English penny to 12 grains sterling silver (0.719 g fine silver) and the introduction of a new <a href="/wiki/Angel_(coin)" title="Angel (coin)">Half-Angel gold coin</a> of 40 grains (2.578 g) worth <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016"/><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">6</span></span>th pound or 40 pence. The latter approximately matched the then-reduced gold content of the Flemish Guilder &amp; the Livre Parisis.\n</p><p>40 pence or <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016"/><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">6</span></span>th pound made up 1 <a href="/wiki/Troy_weight" title="Troy weight">Troy Ounce</a> (480 grains) of sterling silver and would become the model for the German <a href="/wiki/Guldengroschen" title="Guldengroschen">Guldengroschen</a>, also weighing 1 ounce of silver, and divided into 21 <a href="/wiki/Groschen" title="Groschen">groschen</a> (gros, shillings) or 252 <a href="/wiki/Pfennig" title="Pfennig">pfennig</a> (pence).\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Tudor.2C_1551"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Tudor,_1551">Tudor, 1551</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Tudor, 1551">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward_VI_AR_Crown_802157.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Edward_VI_AR_Crown_802157.jpg/220px-Edward_VI_AR_Crown_802157.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="107" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Edward_VI_AR_Crown_802157.jpg/330px-Edward_VI_AR_Crown_802157.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Edward_VI_AR_Crown_802157.jpg/440px-Edward_VI_AR_Crown_802157.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="388" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward_VI_AR_Crown_802157.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Crown (5 shillings) of Edward VI, 1551</div></div></div>\n<p>The last significant depreciation in the pound sterling\xe2\x80\x99s silver standard occurred amidst the 16th century influx of precious metals from the American continent arriving through the <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_Netherlands" title="Habsburg Netherlands">Habsburg Netherlands</a>. The loose enforcement of monetary standards amongst its constituent provinces resulted in a significant <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016"/><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">3</span></span>rd reduction in the bullion content of the pound sterling in 1551, and ultimately to its decoupling from currency values in the Continent.\n</p><p>The troy ounce of sterling silver was henceforth raised in value by 50% from 40 to 60 silver pennies (each penny weighing 8 grains sterling silver &amp; containing 0.4795 g fine silver). The gold half-angel of 40 grains (2.578 g fine gold) was raised in value from 40 pence to 60 pence (5 shillings or <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016"/><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">4</span></span> pound) and was henceforth known as the <a href="/wiki/Crown_(British_coin)" title="Crown (British coin)">Crown</a>.\n</p><p>Prior to 1551, English coin denominations closely matched with corresponding sol (2d) &amp; livre (40d) denominations in the Continent, namely:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Silver <a href="/wiki/Farthing_(English_coin)" title="Farthing (English coin)">farthing</a> (<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016"/><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">4</span></span>d), <a href="/wiki/Halfpenny_(British_pre-decimal_coin)" title="Halfpenny (British pre-decimal coin)">halfpenny</a> (<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016"/><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">2</span></span>d), <a href="/wiki/Penny_(English_coin)" title="Penny (English coin)">penny</a> (1d), <a href="/wiki/Twopence_(British_pre-decimal_coin)" title="Twopence (British pre-decimal coin)">half-groat</a> (2d), &amp; <a href="/wiki/Groat_(coin)" title="Groat (coin)">groat</a> (4d)</li>\n<li>Gold <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016"/><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">4</span></span> angel (20d), <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016"/><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">2</span></span> angel (40d) &amp; <a href="/wiki/Angel_(coin)" title="Angel (coin)">angel</a> (80d)</li></ul>\n<p>After 1551 new denominations were introduced for English circulation only, weighing similar to 1464-issued coins but increased in value 1<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016"/><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">2</span></span> times, namely:\n</p>\n<ul><li>In silver: the <a href="/wiki/Threepence_(British_coin)" title="Threepence (British coin)">Threepence (3d)</a>, replacing the half-groat; the <a href="/wiki/Sixpence_(British_coin)" title="Sixpence (British coin)">Sixpence (6d)</a>, replacing the groat; and a new <a href="/wiki/Shilling_(English_coin)" title="Shilling (English coin)">Shilling</a> or testoon (1/-).</li>\n<li>In silver or gold:  the <a href="/wiki/Half_crown_(British_coin)" title="Half crown (British coin)">Half crown</a> (2/6 or 30d), replacing the <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016"/><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">4</span></span> angel of 20d; and the <a href="/wiki/Crown_(English_coin)" title="Crown (English coin)">Crown</a> (5/- or 60d), replacing the <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016"/><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">2</span></span> angel of 40d.</li>\n<li>And in gold: the new <a href="/wiki/Half_sovereign" title="Half sovereign">Half sovereign</a> (10/-) and <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_(British_coin)" title="Sovereign (British coin)">Sovereign</a> (\xc2\xa31 or 20/-)</li></ul>\n<p>The German monetary system after the <a href="/wiki/Kipper_und_Wipper" title="Kipper und Wipper">Kipper und Wipper</a> Monetary Crisis of the 1620s resembled England\xe2\x80\x99s post-1551 system, with the value of the crown-sized <a href="/wiki/Thaler" title="Thaler">Thaler</a> also revalued from 1 to 1<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016"/><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">2</span></span> silver gulden &amp; with the gulden divided into 240 pfennige or 60 kreuzer.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1601-1816">1601-1816</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: 1601-1816">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1686-Guinea-elephant-and-castle-James-II.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/1686-Guinea-elephant-and-castle-James-II.jpg/220px-1686-Guinea-elephant-and-castle-James-II.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/1686-Guinea-elephant-and-castle-James-II.jpg/330px-1686-Guinea-elephant-and-castle-James-II.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/1686-Guinea-elephant-and-castle-James-II.jpg/440px-1686-Guinea-elephant-and-castle-James-II.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="351" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1686-Guinea-elephant-and-castle-James-II.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Guinea of James II, 1686</div></div></div>\n<p>The silver basis of the pound sterling remained essentially unchanged until the 1816 introduction of the Gold Standard, save for the increase in the number of pennies in a troy ounce from 60 to 62 (hence, 0.464 g fine silver in a penny). Its gold basis remained unsettled, however, until the gold guinea was fixed at 21 shillings in 1717.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Guinea_(coin)" title="Guinea (coin)">guinea</a> was introduced in 1663 with 44<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016"/><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">2</span></span> guineas minted out of 12 troy ounces of 22-karat gold (hence, 7.6885 g fine gold) and initially worth \xc2\xa31 or 20 shillings. While its price in shillings was not legally fixed at first, its persistent trade value above 21 shillings reflected the poor state of clipped underweight silver coins tolerated for payment even for tax dues.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the time of <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Sir Isaac Newton</a>, Master of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mint" title="Royal Mint">Mint</a>, the gold guinea was fixed at 21 shillings in 1717. But without addressing the problem of underweight silver coins, and with the high resulting gold-silver ratio of 15.2, it gave the pound sterling a firmer footing in gold guineas rather than silver pennies, resulting in a de facto <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">gold standard</a>. Silver &amp; copper tokens issued by private entities partly relieved the problem of small change until the <a href="/wiki/Great_Recoinage_of_1816" title="Great Recoinage of 1816">Great Recoinage of 1816</a>.\n</p><p>In line with <a href="/wiki/Gresham%27s_Law" class="mw-redirect" title="Gresham&#39;s Law">Gresham\'s Law</a>, English merchants sent silver abroad in payments, while goods for export were paid for with gold. Scotland, meanwhile, had its own <a href="/wiki/Pound_Scots" title="Pound Scots">Pound Scots</a>. As a consequence of these flows of silver out and gold in, England was effectively on a <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">gold standard</a>. Trade with China aggravated this outflow, as the Chinese refused to accept anything but silver in payment for exports. From the mid-17th century, around 28,000 metric tons (27,600 long tons) of silver were received by China, principally from European powers, in exchange for Chinese tea and other goods. In order to trade with China, England had first to trade with the other European nations to receive silver, which led to the <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> redressing this trade imbalance through the <a href="/wiki/History_of_opium_in_China" title="History of opium in China">indirect sale</a> of <a href="/wiki/Opium" title="Opium">opium</a> to the Chinese.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Domestic demand for silver further reduced silver in circulation, as the improving fortunes of the merchant class led to increased demand for tableware. Silversmiths had always regarded coinage as a source of raw material, already verified for fineness by the government. As a result, sterling coins were being melted and fashioned into sterling silverware at an accelerating rate. An Act of the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">Parliament of England</a> in 1697 tried to stem this tide by raising the minimum acceptable fineness on wrought plate from sterling\'s 92.5% to a new <a href="/wiki/Britannia_silver" title="Britannia silver">Britannia silver</a> standard of 95.83%. Silverware made purely from melted coins would be found wanting when the silversmith took his wares to the <a href="/wiki/Assay_Office" class="mw-redirect" title="Assay Office">Assay Office</a>, thus discouraging the melting of coins.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Establishment_of_modern_currency">Establishment of modern currency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Establishment of modern currency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">Bank of England</a> was founded in 1694, followed by the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_Scotland" title="Bank of Scotland">Bank of Scotland</a> a year later. Both began to issue <a href="/wiki/Paper_money" class="mw-redirect" title="Paper money">paper money</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Currency_of_Great_Britain_.281707.29_and_the_United_Kingdom_.281801.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Currency_of_Great_Britain_(1707)_and_the_United_Kingdom_(1801)">Currency of Great Britain (1707) and the United Kingdom (1801)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Currency of Great Britain (1707) and the United Kingdom (1801)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Pound_Scots" title="Pound Scots">pound Scots</a> once had much the same value as the pound sterling, but it suffered far higher devaluation until in the 17th century it was pegged to sterling at a value of 12 pounds Scots = 1 pound sterling.\n</p><p>In 1707, the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">Kingdom of England</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">Kingdom of Scotland</a> merged to form the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Kingdom of Great Britain</a>. In accordance with the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Union" title="Treaty of Union">Treaty of Union</a>, the currency of Great Britain was sterling, with the pound Scots soon being replaced by sterling at the pegged value.\n</p><p>In 1801, Great Britain and the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Kingdom of Ireland</a> were united to form the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland</a>. However, the <a href="/wiki/Irish_pound" title="Irish pound">Irish pound</a> continued to exist and was not replaced by sterling until January 1826. The conversion rate had long been 13 Irish pounds to 12 pounds sterling. The Irish pound was readopted in 1928, six years after the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Irish_Treaty" title="Anglo-Irish Treaty">Anglo-Irish Treaty</a> restored Irish independence.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_in_the_Empire">Use in the Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Use in the Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sterling_area" title="Sterling area">Sterling area</a></div>\n<p>Sterling circulated in much of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>. In some parts, it was used alongside local currencies. For example, the gold sovereign was legal tender in Canada despite the use of the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_dollar" title="Canadian dollar">Canadian dollar</a>. Several colonies and dominions adopted the pound as their own currency. These included Australia, <a href="/wiki/Barbados" title="Barbados">Barbados</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/British_West_Africa" title="British West Africa">British West Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fiji" title="Fiji">Fiji</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_India" class="mw-redirect" title="British India">British India</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Free_State" title="Irish Free State">Irish Free State</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">Jamaica</a>, New Zealand, South Africa and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Rhodesia" title="Southern Rhodesia">Southern Rhodesia</a>. Some of these retained parity with sterling throughout their existence (e.g. the <a href="/wiki/South_African_pound" title="South African pound">South African pound</a>), while others deviated from parity after the end of the gold standard (e.g. the <a href="/wiki/Australian_pound" title="Australian pound">Australian pound</a>). These currencies and others tied to sterling constituted the <a href="/wiki/Sterling_area" title="Sterling area">sterling area</a>.\n</p><p>The original English colonies on mainland North America were not party to the sterling area because the above-mentioned silver shortage in England coincided with these colonies\' formative years. As a result of equitable trade (and rather less equitable piracy), the Spanish milled dollar became the most common coin within the English colonies.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gold_standard">Gold standard</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Gold standard">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sovereign_Victoria_1842_662015.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Sovereign_Victoria_1842_662015.jpg/220px-Sovereign_Victoria_1842_662015.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="111" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e0/Sovereign_Victoria_1842_662015.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="325" data-file-height="164" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sovereign_Victoria_1842_662015.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Shield reverse" sovereign of Queen Victoria, 1842</div></div></div>\n<p>During the American war of independence and the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_wars" class="mw-redirect" title="Napoleonic wars">Napoleonic wars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">Bank of England</a> notes were <a href="/wiki/Legal_tender" title="Legal tender">legal tender</a>, and their value floated relative to gold. The Bank also issued silver tokens to alleviate the shortage of silver coins. In 1816, the <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">gold standard</a> was adopted officially, with silver coins minted at a rate of 66 shillings to a troy pound of sterling silver, thus rendering them as "token" issues (i.e. not containing their value in precious metal). In 1817, the <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_(British_coin)" title="Sovereign (British coin)">sovereign</a> was introduced, valued at 20 shillings. Struck in 22\xe2\x80\x91karat gold, it contained 113 grains or 7.32238 grams of fine gold and replaced the <a href="/wiki/Guinea_(coin)" title="Guinea (coin)">guinea</a> as the standard British gold coin without changing the gold standard. In 1825, the <a href="/wiki/Irish_pound" title="Irish pound">Irish pound</a>, which had been pegged to sterling since 1801 at a rate of 13 Irish pounds = 12 pounds sterling, was replaced, at the same rate, with sterling.\n</p><p>By the 19th century, the pound sterling was widely accepted outside Britain. The American <a href="/wiki/Nellie_Bly" title="Nellie Bly">Nellie Bly</a> carried Bank of England notes on <a href="/wiki/Around_the_World_in_Seventy-Two_Days" title="Around the World in Seventy-Two Days">her 1889\xe2\x80\x931890 trip around the world in 72 days</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bly1890_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bly1890-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> During the late 19th and early 20th centuries, many other countries adopted the gold standard. As a consequence, conversion rates between different currencies could be determined simply from the respective gold standards. The pound sterling was equal to 4.87 United States dollars, 4.87 <a href="/wiki/Canadian_dollar" title="Canadian dollar">Canadian dollars</a>, 12.11 <a href="/wiki/Dutch_guilder" title="Dutch guilder">Dutch guilders</a>, 25.22 <a href="/wiki/French_franc" title="French franc">French francs</a> (or equivalent currencies in the <a href="/wiki/Latin_Monetary_Union" title="Latin Monetary Union">Latin Monetary Union</a>), 20.43 <a href="/wiki/German_gold_mark" title="German gold mark">German marks</a>, 9.46 <a href="/wiki/Ruble" title="Ruble">Russian Rubles</a> or 24.02 <a href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian_krone" title="Austro-Hungarian krone">Austro-Hungarian krone</a>. After the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Conferences" class="mw-redirect" title="International Monetary Conferences">International Monetary Conference of 1867</a> in Paris, the possibility of the UK joining the <a href="/wiki/Latin_Monetary_Union" title="Latin Monetary Union">Latin Monetary Union</a> was discussed, and a <a href="/wiki/Royal_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Commission">Royal Commission</a> on International Coinage examined the issues,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> resulting in a decision against joining monetary union.\n</p><p>The gold standard was suspended at the outbreak of the war in 1914, with Bank of England and Treasury notes becoming legal tender. Before <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, the United Kingdom had one of the world\'s strongest economies, holding 40% of the world\'s overseas investments. But after the end of the war, the country was indebted: Britain owed \xc2\xa3850&#160;million (\xc2\xa337.3&#160;billion as of 2015)<sup id="cite_ref-inflation-UK_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-UK-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> with interest costing the country some 40% of all government spending.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> To try to resume stability, a version of the gold standard was reintroduced in 1925, under which the currency was fixed to gold at its pre-war peg, but one could only exchange currency for gold bullion, not for coins. This was abandoned on 21 September 1931, during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>, and sterling suffered an initial devaluation of some 25%.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bretton_Woods">Bretton Woods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Bretton Woods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Economic_history_of_the_United_Kingdom#1945\xe2\x80\x931951:_Age_of_Austerity" title="Economic history of the United Kingdom">Economic history of the United Kingdom \xc2\xa7&#160;1945\xe2\x80\x931951: Age of Austerity</a></div>\n<p>In 1940, an agreement with the US pegged the pound to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">U.S. dollar</a> at a rate of \xc2\xa31 = $4.03. (Only the year before, it had been $4.86.)<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> This rate was maintained through the <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">Second World War</a> and became part of the <a href="/wiki/Bretton_Woods_system" title="Bretton Woods system">Bretton Woods system</a> which governed post-war exchange rates. Under continuing economic pressure, and despite months of denials that it would do so, on 19 September 1949 the government devalued the pound by 30.5% to $2.80.<sup id="cite_ref-srplumt_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-srplumt-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The move prompted several other currencies to be devalued against the dollar.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Operation_Bernhard" title="Operation Bernhard">Operation Bernhard</a> was the codename of a secret Nazi plan devised during the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a> by the <a href="/wiki/RSHA" class="mw-redirect" title="RSHA">RSHA</a> and the <a href="/wiki/SS" class="mw-redirect" title="SS">SS</a> to destabilise the British economy via economic warfare by flooding the global economy and the British Empire with forged Bank of England \xc2\xa35, \xc2\xa310, \xc2\xa320, and \xc2\xa350 notes.\n</p><p>In 1961, 1964, and 1966, the pound came under renewed pressure, as speculators were selling pounds for dollars. In summer 1966, with the value of the pound falling in the currency markets, exchange controls were tightened by the <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Wilson</a> government. Among the measures, tourists were banned from taking more than \xc2\xa350 out of the country in travellers\' cheques and remittances, plus \xc2\xa315 in cash; this restriction was not lifted until 1979. The pound was devalued by 14.3% to $2.40 on 18 November 1967.<sup id="cite_ref-srplumt_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-srplumt-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-srdev67_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-srdev67-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decimalisation">Decimalisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Decimalisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Decimal_Day" title="Decimal Day">Decimal Day</a></div>\n<p>Until decimalisation, amounts were stated in pounds, shillings, and pence, with various widely understood notations. The same amount could be stated as 32s 6d, 32/6, \xc2\xa31 12s 6d, or \xc2\xa31/12/6. It was customary to specify some prices (for example professional fees and auction prices for works of art) in guineas (one guinea was 21 shillings) although <a href="/wiki/Guinea_(British_coin)" class="mw-redirect" title="Guinea (British coin)">guinea coins</a> were no longer in use.\n</p><p>Formal parliamentary proposals to decimalise sterling were first made in 1824 when <a href="/wiki/John_Wrottesley,_1st_Baron_Wrottesley" title="John Wrottesley, 1st Baron Wrottesley">Sir John Wrottesley</a>, MP for <a href="/wiki/Staffordshire_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Staffordshire (UK Parliament constituency)">Staffordshire</a>, asked in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">British House of Commons</a> whether consideration had been given to decimalising the currency.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Wrottesley raised the issue in the House of Commons again in 1833,<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> and it was again raised by <a href="/wiki/John_Bowring" title="John Bowring">John Bowring</a>, MP for <a href="/wiki/Kilmarnock_Burghs_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Kilmarnock Burghs (UK Parliament constituency)">Kilmarnock Burghs</a>, in 1847<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> whose efforts led to the introduction in 1848 of what was in effect the first decimal coin in the United Kingdom, the florin, valued at one-tenth of a pound sterling. However, full decimalisation was resisted, although the florin coin, re-designated as <i>ten new pence</i>, survived the transfer to a full decimal system in 1971, with examples surviving in British coinage until 1993.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/John_Benjamin_Smith" title="John Benjamin Smith">John Benjamin Smith</a>, MP for <a href="/wiki/Stirling_Burghs_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Stirling Burghs (UK Parliament constituency)">Stirling Burghs</a>, raised the issue of full decimalisation again in Parliament in 1853,<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> resulting in the Chancellor of the Exchequer, <a href="/wiki/William_Gladstone" class="mw-redirect" title="William Gladstone">William Gladstone</a>, announcing soon afterwards that "the great question of a decimal coinage" was "now under serious consideration".<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> A full proposal for the decimalisation of sterling was then tabled in the House of Commons in June 1855, by <a href="/wiki/Sir_William_Brown,_1st_Baronet,_of_Richmond_Hill" title="Sir William Brown, 1st Baronet, of Richmond Hill">William Brown</a>, MP for <a href="/wiki/South_Lancashire_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="South Lancashire (UK Parliament constituency)">Lancashire Southern</a>, with the suggestion that the pound sterling be divided into one thousand parts, each called a "mil", or alternatively a farthing, as the pound was then equivalent to 960 farthings which could easily be rounded up to one thousand farthings in the new system.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> This did not result in the conversion of the pound sterling into a decimal system, but it was agreed to establish a <a href="/wiki/Royal_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Commission">Royal Commission</a> to look into the issue.<sup
