id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> However, largely due to the hostility to decimalisation of two of the appointed commissioners, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Jones-Loyd,_1st_Baron_Overstone" title="Samuel Jones-Loyd, 1st Baron Overstone">Lord Overstone</a> (a banker) and <a href="/wiki/John_Hubbard,_1st_Baron_Addington" title="John Hubbard, 1st Baron Addington">John Hubbard</a> (Governor of the Bank of England), decimalisation in Britain was effectively quashed for over a hundred years.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, the pound sterling was decimalised in various British colonial territories before the United Kingdom (and in several cases in line with William Brown\'s proposal that the pound be divided into 1,000 parts, called mils). These included Hong Kong from 1863 to 1866;<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Cyprus from 1955 until 1960 (and continued on the island as the division of the <a href="/wiki/Cypriot_pound" title="Cypriot pound">Cypriot pound</a> until 1983); and the Palestine Mandate from 1926 until 1948.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Towards the end of the Second World War, various attempts to decimalise the pound sterling in the United Kingdom were made.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Later, in 1966, the British government decided to include in the <a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_Speech" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen&#39;s Speech">Queen\'s Speech</a> a plan to convert the pound into a decimal currency.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> As a result of this, on 15 February 1971, the UK decimalised the pound sterling, replacing the shilling and the penny with a single subdivision, the new penny. For example, a price tag of \xc2\xa31 12s 6d became <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016"/><span class="frac" role="math">\xc2\xa31.62<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">2</span></span>. The word "new" was omitted from coins minted after 1981.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Free-floating_pound">Free-floating pound</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Free-floating pound">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>With the breakdown of the <a href="/wiki/Bretton_Woods_system" title="Bretton Woods system">Bretton Woods system</a>, the pound <a href="/wiki/Floating_currency" class="mw-redirect" title="Floating currency">floated</a> from August 1971 onwards. At first, it appreciated a little, rising to almost $2.65 in March 1972 from $2.42, the upper bound of the band in which it had been fixed. The <a href="/wiki/Sterling_area" title="Sterling area">sterling area</a> effectively ended at this time, when the majority of its members also chose to float freely against the pound and the dollar.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1976_sterling_crisis">1976 sterling crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: 1976 sterling crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/James_Callaghan" title="James Callaghan">James Callaghan</a> became Prime Minister in 1976. He was immediately told the economy was facing huge problems, according to documents released in 2006 by the <a href="/wiki/National_Archives" class="mw-redirect" title="National Archives">National Archives</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The effects of the <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">1973 oil crisis</a> were still being felt, with inflation rising to nearly 27% in 1975.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Financial markets were beginning to believe the pound was overvalued, and in April that year <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal" title="The Wall Street Journal">The Wall Street Journal</a></i> advised the sale of sterling investments in the face of high taxes, in a story that ended with "goodbye, Great Britain. It was nice knowing you".<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> At the time the UK government was running a budget deficit, and Labour\'s strategy emphasised high public spending.<sup id="cite_ref-srplumt_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-srplumt-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Callaghan was told there were three possible outcomes: a disastrous free fall in sterling, an internationally unacceptable siege economy, or a deal with key allies to prop up the pound while painful economic reforms were put in place. The US government feared the crisis could endanger NATO and the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Economic Community</a> (EEC), and in light of this the US Treasury set out to force domestic policy changes. In November 1976 the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a> (IMF) announced the conditions for a loan, including deep cuts in <a href="/wiki/Public_expenditure" title="Public expenditure">public expenditure</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="1979.E2.80.931989"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1979\xe2\x80\x931989">1979\xe2\x80\x931989</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: 1979\xe2\x80\x931989">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Conservative Party was elected to office in 1979, on a programme of fiscal austerity. Initially, the pound rocketed, moving above US$2.40, as interest rates rose in response to the <a href="/wiki/Monetarist" class="mw-redirect" title="Monetarist">monetarist</a> policy of targeting <a href="/wiki/Money_supply" title="Money supply">money supply</a>. The high exchange rate was widely blamed for the deep <a href="/wiki/Recession" title="Recession">recession</a> of 1981. Sterling fell sharply after 1980; at its lowest, the pound stood at just $1.03 in March 1985, before rising to $1.70 in December 1989.<sup id="cite_ref-Samson_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Samson-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Following_the_Deutsche_Mark">Following the Deutsche Mark</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Following the Deutsche Mark">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1988, <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nigel_Lawson" title="Nigel Lawson">Nigel Lawson</a>, decided that the pound should "shadow" the West German <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Mark" title="Deutsche Mark">Deutsche Mark</a> (DM), with the unintended result of a rapid rise in inflation as the economy boomed due to low interest rates. (For ideological reasons, the Conservative Government declined to use alternative mechanisms to control the explosion of credit. For this reason, former Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a> referred to Lawson as a "one club golfer".)<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following <a href="/wiki/German_reunification" title="German reunification">German reunification</a> in 1990, the reverse held true, as high German borrowing costs to fund Eastern reconstruction, exacerbated by the political decision to convert the <a href="/wiki/East_German_mark" title="East German mark">Ostmark</a> to the DM on a 1:1 basis, meant that interest rates in other countries shadowing the DM, especially the UK, were far too high relative to domestic circumstances, leading to a housing decline and recession.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Following_the_European_Currency_Unit">Following the European Currency Unit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Following the European Currency Unit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>On 8 October 1990 the Conservative government (<a href="/wiki/Third_Thatcher_ministry" title="Third Thatcher ministry">Third Thatcher ministry</a>) decided to join the <a href="/wiki/European_Exchange_Rate_Mechanism" title="European Exchange Rate Mechanism">European Exchange Rate Mechanism</a> (ERM), with the pound set at <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Mark" title="Deutsche Mark">DM</a>2.95. However, the country was forced to withdraw from the system on "<a href="/wiki/Black_Wednesday" title="Black Wednesday">Black Wednesday</a>" (16 September 1992) as Britain\'s economic performance made the exchange rate unsustainable.\n</p><p>"Black Wednesday" saw interest rates jump from 10% to 15% in an unsuccessful attempt to stop the pound from falling below the ERM limits. The exchange rate fell to DM2.20. Those who had argued<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> for a lower GBP/DM exchange rate were vindicated since the cheaper pound encouraged exports and contributed to the economic prosperity of the 1990s.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Following_inflation_targets">Following inflation targets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Following inflation targets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1997, the newly elected <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour</a> government handed over day-to-day control of interest rates to the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">Bank of England</a> (a policy that had originally been advocated by the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrats</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The Bank is now responsible for setting its base rate of interest so as to keep inflation (as measured by the <a href="/wiki/Consumer_Price_Index_(United_Kingdom)" title="Consumer Price Index (United Kingdom)">Consumer Price Index</a> (CPI)) very close to 2% per annum. Should CPI inflation be more than one percentage point above or below the target, the governor of the Bank of England is required to write an open letter to the <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a> explaining the reasons for this and the measures which will be taken to bring this measure of inflation back in line with the 2% target. On 17 April 2007, annual CPI inflation was reported at 3.1% (inflation of the <a href="/wiki/Retail_Prices_Index_(United_Kingdom)" class="mw-redirect" title="Retail Prices Index (United Kingdom)">Retail Prices Index</a> was 4.8%). Accordingly, and for the first time, the Governor had to write publicly to the government explaining why inflation was more than one percentage point higher than its target.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Euro">Euro</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Euro">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_and_the_euro" title="United Kingdom and the euro">United Kingdom and the euro</a></div>\n<p>In 2007, <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Brown" title="Gordon Brown">Gordon Brown</a>, then <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a>, ruled out membership in the eurozone for the foreseeable future, saying that the decision not to join had been right for Britain and for Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 1 January 2008, with the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Cyprus" class="mw-redirect" title="Republic of Cyprus">Republic of Cyprus</a> switching its currency from the <a href="/wiki/Cypriot_pound" title="Cypriot pound">Cypriot pound</a> to the euro, the British sovereign bases on Cyprus (<a href="/wiki/Akrotiri_and_Dhekelia" title="Akrotiri and Dhekelia">Akrotiri and Dhekelia</a>) followed suit, making the Sovereign Base Areas the only territory under British sovereignty to officially use the euro.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The government of former Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a> had pledged to hold a public referendum to decide on the adoption of the Euro should "<a href="/wiki/Five_economic_tests" title="Five economic tests">five economic tests</a>" be met, to increase the likelihood that any adoption of the euro would be in the national interest. In addition to these internal (national) criteria, the UK would have to meet the European Union\'s economic <a href="/wiki/Convergence_criteria" class="mw-redirect" title="Convergence criteria">convergence criteria</a> (Maastricht criteria) before being allowed to adopt the euro. The Conservative and Liberal Democrat coalition government (2010\xe2\x80\x932015) ruled out joining the euro for that parliamentary term.\n</p><p>The idea of replacing the pound with the euro was always controversial with the British public, partly because of the pound\'s identity as a symbol of British sovereignty and because it would, according to some critics, have led to suboptimal interest rates, harming the British economy.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> In December 2008, the results of a BBC poll of 1000 people suggested that 71% would vote no to the euro, 23% would vote yes, while 6% said they were unsure.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> The pound did not join the Second European Exchange Rate Mechanism (ERM II) after the euro was created. <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> and the UK had opt-outs from entry to the euro. Theoretically, every EU nation but Denmark must eventually sign up.\n</p><p>As a member of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, the United Kingdom could have adopted the <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">euro</a> as its currency. However, the subject was always politically controversial, and the UK negotiated an opt-out on this issue. Following the <a href="/wiki/Brexit" title="Brexit">UK\'s withdrawal from the EU</a>, on 31 January 2020, the Bank of England ended its membership of the European System of Central Banks,<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> and shares in the <a href="/wiki/European_Central_Bank" title="European Central Bank">European Central Bank</a> were reallocated to other EU banks.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_exchange_rates">Recent exchange rates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Recent exchange rates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_the_United_Kingdom#Exchange_rates" title="Economy of the United Kingdom">Economy of the United Kingdom \xc2\xa7&#160;Exchange rates</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:370px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GBP-USD_v2.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/GBP-USD_v2.svg/368px-GBP-USD_v2.svg.png" decoding="async" width="368" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/GBP-USD_v2.svg/552px-GBP-USD_v2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/GBP-USD_v2.svg/736px-GBP-USD_v2.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GBP-USD_v2.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The cost of one pound in <a href="/wiki/US_dollar" class="mw-redirect" title="US dollar">US dollars</a> (from 1990)</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:370px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Euro_exchange_rate_to_GBP.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Euro_exchange_rate_to_GBP.svg/368px-Euro_exchange_rate_to_GBP.svg.png" decoding="async" width="368" height="184" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Euro_exchange_rate_to_GBP.svg/552px-Euro_exchange_rate_to_GBP.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Euro_exchange_rate_to_GBP.svg/736px-Euro_exchange_rate_to_GBP.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Euro_exchange_rate_to_GBP.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The cost of one <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">Euro</a> in pounds (from 1999)</div></div></div>\n<p>The pound and the euro fluctuate in value against one another, although there may be correlation between movements in their respective exchange rates with other currencies such as the US dollar. Inflation concerns in the UK led the Bank of England to raise interest rates in late 2006 and 2007. This caused the pound to appreciate against other major currencies and, with the US dollar depreciating at the same time, the pound hit a 15-year high against the US dollar on 18 April 2007, reaching US$2 the day before, for the first time since 1992. The pound and many other currencies continued to appreciate against the dollar; sterling hit a 26-year high of US$2.1161 on 7 November 2007 as the dollar fell worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> From mid-2003 to mid-2007, the pound/euro rate remained within a narrow range (\xe2\x82\xac1.45 \xc2\xb1 5%).<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Global_financial_crisis_of_2008%E2%80%932009" class="mw-redirect" title="Global financial crisis of 2008\xe2\x80\x932009">global financial crisis in late 2008</a>, the pound depreciated sharply, reaching $1.38 (US) on 23 January 2009<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> and falling below \xe2\x82\xac1.25 against the euro in April 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> There was a further decline during the remainder of 2008, most dramatically on 29 December when its euro rate hit an all-time low at \xe2\x82\xac1.0219, while its US dollar rate depreciated.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The pound appreciated in early 2009, reaching a peak against the euro of \xe2\x82\xac1.17 in mid-July. In the following months the pound remained broadly steady against the euro, with the pound\'s valued on 27 May 2011 at \xe2\x82\xac1.15 and US$1.65.\n</p><p>On 5 March 2009, the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">Bank of England</a> announced that it would pump \xc2\xa375&#160;billion of new <a href="/wiki/Capital_(economics)" title="Capital (economics)">capital</a> into the <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Economy of the United Kingdom">British economy</a>, through a process known as <a href="/wiki/Quantitative_easing" title="Quantitative easing">quantitative easing</a> (QE). This was the first time in the United Kingdom\'s history that this measure had been used, although the Bank\'s <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_the_Bank_of_England" title="Governor of the Bank of England">Governor</a> <a href="/wiki/Mervyn_King,_Baron_King_of_Lothbury" title="Mervyn King, Baron King of Lothbury">Mervyn King</a> suggested it was not an experiment.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The process saw the Bank of England creating new money for itself, which it then used to purchase <a href="/wiki/Asset" title="Asset">assets</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Government_bond" title="Government bond">government bonds</a>, secured <a href="/wiki/Commercial_paper" title="Commercial paper">commercial paper</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Corporate_bond" title="Corporate bond">corporate bonds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CNN1_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN1-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The initial amount stated to be created through this method was \xc2\xa375&#160;billion, although <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a> <a href="/wiki/Alistair_Darling" title="Alistair Darling">Alistair Darling</a> had given permission for up to \xc2\xa3150&#160;billion to be created if necessary.<sup id="cite_ref-Times_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Times-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> It was expected that the process would continue for three months, with results only likely in the long term.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> By 5 November 2009, some \xc2\xa3175&#160;billion had been injected using QE, and the process remained less effective in the long term. In July 2012, the final increase in QE meant it had peaked at \xc2\xa3375&#160;billion, then holding solely UK Government bonds, representing one third of the UK national debt.<sup id="cite_ref-BofEAPF_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BofEAPF-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The result of the <a href="/wiki/2016_United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum" title="2016 United Kingdom European Union membership referendum">2016 UK referendum on EU membership</a> caused a major decline in the pound against other world currencies as the future of international trade relationships and domestic political leadership became unclear.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> The referendum result weakened sterling against the euro by 5% overnight. The night before the vote, the pound was trading at \xe2\x82\xac1.30; the next day, this had fallen to \xe2\x82\xac1.23. By October 2016, the exchange rate was \xe2\x82\xac1.12 to the pound, a fall of 14% since the referendum. By the end of August 2017 the pound was even lower, at \xe2\x82\xac1.08.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Against the US dollar, meanwhile, the pound fell from $1.466 to $1.3694 when the referendum result was first revealed, and down to $1.2232 by October 2016, a fall of 16%.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Annual_inflation_rate">Annual inflation rate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Annual inflation rate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Bank of England had stated in 2009 that the decision had been taken to prevent the rate of <a href="/wiki/Inflation" title="Inflation">inflation</a> falling below the 2% target rate.<sup id="cite_ref-CNN1_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN1-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Mervyn King, the Governor of the Bank of England, had also suggested there were no other monetary options left, as <a href="/wiki/Interest_rates" class="mw-redirect" title="Interest rates">interest rates</a> had already been cut to their lowest level ever (0.5%) and it was unlikely that they would be cut further.<sup id="cite_ref-Times_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Times-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The inflation rate rose in following years, reaching 5.2% per year (based on the Consumer Price Index) in September 2011, then decreased to around 2.5% the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Coins">Coins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Coins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coins_of_the_pound_sterling" title="Coins of the pound sterling">Coins of the pound sterling</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-decimal_coins">Pre-decimal coins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Pre-decimal coins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The silver penny (plural: <i>pence</i>; abbreviation: <i>d</i>) was the principal and often the only coin in circulation from the 8th century until the 13th century. Although some fractions of the penny were struck (see <a href="/wiki/Farthing_(British_coin)" title="Farthing (British coin)">farthing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Halfpenny_(British_coin)" class="mw-redirect" title="Halfpenny (British coin)">halfpenny</a>), it was more common to find pennies cut into halves and quarters to provide smaller change. Very few gold coins were struck, with the <a href="/wiki/Gold_penny" title="Gold penny">gold penny</a> (worth 20 silver pence) a rare example. However, in 1279, the <i><a href="/wiki/Groat_(coin)" title="Groat (coin)">groat</a></i>, worth 4d, was introduced, with the half groat following in 1344. 1344 also saw the establishment of a gold coinage with the introduction (after the failed <a href="/wiki/Florin_(English_coin)" title="Florin (English coin)">gold florin</a>) of the <i><a href="/wiki/Noble_(English_coin)" title="Noble (English coin)">noble</a></i> worth six shillings and eight pence (6/8) (i.e. 3 nobles to the pound), together with the half and quarter noble. Reforms in 1464 saw a reduction in value of the coinage in both silver and gold, with the noble renamed the <i>ryal</i> and worth 10/\xe2\x80\x93 (i.e. 2 to the pound) and the <i><a href="/wiki/Angel_(coin)" title="Angel (coin)">angel</a></i> introduced at the noble\'s old value of 6/8.\n</p><p>The reign of <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">Henry VII</a> saw the introduction of two important coins: the <a href="/wiki/Shilling" title="Shilling">shilling</a> (abbr.: <i>s</i>; known as the <i>testoon</i>, equivalent to twelve pence) in 1487 and the pound (known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Sovereign_(British_coin)" title="Sovereign (British coin)">sovereign</a></i>, abbr.: <i>\xc2\xa3</i> or <i>L</i>, equivalent to twenty shillings) in 1489. In 1526, several new denominations of gold coins were added, including the <i><a href="/wiki/Crown_(British_coin)" title="Crown (British coin)">crown</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Half_crown_(British_coin)" title="Half crown (British coin)">half crown</a></i>, worth five shillings (<i>5/\xe2\x80\x93</i>) and two shillings and six pence (<i>2/6</i>, <i>two and six</i>) respectively. <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII</a>\'s reign (1509\xe2\x80\x931547) saw a high level of <a href="/wiki/Debasement" title="Debasement">debasement</a> which continued into the reign of <a href="/wiki/Edward_VI" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward VI">Edward VI</a> (1547\xe2\x80\x931553). This debasement was halted in 1552, and new silver coinage was introduced, including coins for 1d, 2d, <a href="/wiki/Threepence_(British_coin)" title="Threepence (British coin)">3d</a>, 4d and <a href="/wiki/Sixpence_(British_coin)" title="Sixpence (British coin)">6d</a>, 1/\xe2\x80\x93, 2/6 and 5/\xe2\x80\x93. In the reign of <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I" title="Elizabeth I">Elizabeth I</a> (1558\xe2\x80\x931603), silver <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016"/><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">3</span>&#8260;<span class="den">4</span></span>d and <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016"/><span class="frac" role="math">1<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">2</span></span>d coins were added, but these denominations did not last. Gold coins included the half-crown, crown, angel, half-sovereign (10/\xe2\x80\x93) and sovereign (\xc2\xa31). Elizabeth\'s reign also saw the introduction of the horse-drawn screw press to produce the first "milled" coins.\n</p><p>Following the succession of the Scottish King <a href="/wiki/James_VI" class="mw-redirect" title="James VI">James VI</a> to the English throne, a new gold coinage was introduced, including the <i><a href="/wiki/Spur_ryal" title="Spur ryal">spur ryal</a></i> (15/\xe2\x80\x93), the <i><a href="/wiki/Unite_(English_coin)" title="Unite (English coin)">unite</a></i> (20/\xe2\x80\x93) and the <i>rose ryal</i> (30/\xe2\x80\x93). The <i><a href="/wiki/Laurel_(English_coin)" title="Laurel (English coin)">laurel</a></i>, worth 20/\xe2\x80\x93, followed in 1619. The first base metal coins were also introduced: <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> <a href="/wiki/Farthing_(British_coin)" title="Farthing (British coin)">farthings</a>. Copper <a href="/wiki/Halfpenny_(British_coin)" class="mw-redirect" title="Halfpenny (British coin)">halfpenny</a> coins followed in the reign of <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">Charles I</a>. During the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a>, a number of siege coinages were produced, often in unusual denominations.\n</p><p>Following the restoration of the monarchy in 1660, the coinage was reformed, with the ending of production of hammered coins in 1662. The <i><a href="/wiki/Guinea_(British_coin)" class="mw-redirect" title="Guinea (British coin)">guinea</a></i> was introduced in 1663, soon followed by the <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016"/><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">2</span></span>, 2 and 5 guinea coins. The silver coinage consisted of denominations of 1d, 2d, 3d, 4d and 6d, 1/\xe2\x80\x93, 2/6 and 5/\xe2\x80\x93. Due to the widespread export of silver in the 18th century, the production of silver coins gradually came to a halt, with the half crown and crown not issued after the 1750s, the 6d and 1/\xe2\x80\x93 stopping production in the 1780s. In response, copper 1d and 2d coins and a gold <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016"/><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">3</span></span> guinea (7/\xe2\x80\x93) were introduced in 1797. The copper penny was the only one of these coins to survive long.\n</p><p>To alleviate the shortage of silver coins, between 1797 and 1804, the Bank of England counterstamped <a href="/wiki/Spanish_dollar" title="Spanish dollar">Spanish dollars</a> (8 reales) and other <a href="/wiki/Spanish_real" title="Spanish real">Spanish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spanish_colonial_real" title="Spanish colonial real">Spanish colonial</a> coins for circulation. A small counterstamp of the King\'s head was used. Until 1800, these circulated at a rate of 4/9 for 8 reales. After 1800, a rate of 5/- for 8 reales was used. The Bank then issued silver tokens for 5/\xe2\x80\x93 (struck over Spanish dollars) in 1804, followed by tokens for 1/6 and 3/\xe2\x80\x93 between 1811 and 1816.\n</p><p>In 1816, a new silver coinage was introduced in denominations of 6d, 1/\xe2\x80\x93, 2/6 (half-crown) and 5/\xe2\x80\x93 (crown). The crown was only issued intermittently until 1900. It was followed by a new gold coinage in 1817 consisting of 10/\xe2\x80\x93 and \xc2\xa31 coins, known as the half sovereign and <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_(British_coin)" title="Sovereign (British coin)">sovereign</a>. The silver 4d coin was reintroduced in 1836, followed by the 3d in 1838, with the 4d coin issued only for colonial use after 1855. In 1848, the 2/\xe2\x80\x93 <i><a href="/wiki/Florin_(British_coin)" title="Florin (British coin)">florin</a></i> was introduced, followed by the short-lived <a href="/wiki/Double_florin" title="Double florin">double florin</a> in 1887. In 1860, copper was replaced by bronze in the farthing (quarter penny, <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016"/><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">4</span></span>d), halfpenny and penny.\n</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">First World War</a>, production of the <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_(British_coin)" title="Sovereign (British coin)">sovereign</a> and <a href="/wiki/Half-sovereign" class="mw-redirect" title="Half-sovereign">half-sovereign</a> was suspended, and although the <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">gold standard</a> was later restored, the coins saw little circulation thereafter. In 1920, the silver standard, maintained at .925 since 1552, was reduced to .500. In 1937, a nickel-brass 3d coin was introduced; the last silver 3d coins were issued seven years later. In 1947, the remaining silver coins were replaced with <a href="/wiki/Cupro-nickel" class="mw-redirect" title="Cupro-nickel">cupro-nickel</a>, with the exception of <a href="/wiki/Royal_Maundy#Maundy_coinage" title="Royal Maundy">Maundy coinage</a> which was then restored to .925. Inflation caused the farthing to cease production in 1956 and be demonetised in 1960. In the run-up to decimalisation, the halfpenny and half-crown were demonetised in 1969.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decimal_coins">Decimal coins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Decimal coins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table style="border: 1px #cccccc solid; border-collapse: collapse; background: #f9f9f9; float:right; clear:right; margin:0.3em 0 0.3em 1em;">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th colspan="2" style="width:256px;padding:2px;"><a href="/wiki/One_pound_(British_decimal_coin)" class="mw-redirect" title="One pound (British decimal coin)">\xc2\xa31</a> coin (new design, 2016)\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="padding:0px; border: 1px #cccccc solid;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_12_sided_pound_coin.png" class="image"><img alt="British 12 sided pound coin.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c0/British_12_sided_pound_coin.png/130px-British_12_sided_pound_coin.png" decoding="async" width="130" height="128" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/c/c0/British_12_sided_pound_coin.png 1.5x" data-file-width="189" data-file-height="186" /></a><a href="/wiki/File:British_12_sided_pound_coin_reverse.png" class="image"><img alt="British 12 sided pound coin reverse.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/60/British_12_sided_pound_coin_reverse.png/130px-British_12_sided_pound_coin_reverse.png" decoding="async" width="130" height="128" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/6/60/British_12_sided_pound_coin_reverse.png 1.5x" data-file-width="184" data-file-height="181" /></a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="font-size: 85%; vertical-align: top;">\n<td style="width:126px;padding:2px; border: 1px #cccccc solid; line-height: 1.5em;"><a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Elizabeth II</a>\n</td>\n<td style="width:126px;padding:2px 1px 2px 2px; border: 1px #cccccc solid; line-height: 1.5em;">English <a href="/wiki/Tudor_rose" title="Tudor rose">rose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leek#Cultural_significance" title="Leek">Welsh leek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thistle#Scottish_thistle" title="Thistle">Scottish thistle</a>, and Northern Irish <a href="/wiki/Shamrock" title="Shamrock">shamrock</a>.\n</td></tr>\n\n</tbody></table>\n<p>British coinage timeline:\n</p>\n<ul><li>1968: The first <a href="/wiki/Coins_of_the_pound_sterling" title="Coins of the pound sterling">decimal coins</a> were introduced. These were <a href="/wiki/Cupro-nickel" class="mw-redirect" title="Cupro-nickel">cupro-nickel</a> <a href="/wiki/Five_pence_(British_decimal_coin)" class="mw-redirect" title="Five pence (British decimal coin)">5p</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ten_pence_(British_decimal_coin)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ten pence (British decimal coin)">10p coins</a> which were the same size as, equivalent in value to, and circulated alongside, the <a href="/wiki/Shilling_(British_coin)" title="Shilling (British coin)">one shilling coin</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Florin_(British_coin)" title="Florin (British coin)">florin (two shilling coin)</a> respectively.</li>\n<li>1969: The curved equilateral <a href="/wiki/Heptagon" title="Heptagon">heptagonal</a> cupro-nickel <a href="/wiki/Fifty_pence_(British_decimal_coin)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fifty pence (British decimal coin)">50p coin</a> replaced the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England_10s_note" title="Bank of England 10s note">ten shilling banknote</a> (10/\xe2\x80\x93).</li>\n<li>1970: The <a href="/wiki/Half_crown_(British_coin)" title="Half crown (British coin)">Half crown</a> (2/6, 12.5p) was <a href="/wiki/Demonetised" class="mw-redirect" title="Demonetised">demonetised</a>.</li>\n<li>1971: The decimal coinage was completed when decimalisation came into effect in 1971 with the introduction of the bronze <a href="/wiki/Halfpenny_(British_decimal_coin)" title="Halfpenny (British decimal coin)">half new penny</a> (<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016"/><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">2</span></span>p), <a href="/wiki/Penny_(British_decimal_coin)" title="Penny (British decimal coin)">new penny</a> (1p), and <a href="/wiki/Two_pence_(British_decimal_coin)" title="Two pence (British decimal coin)">two new pence</a> (2p) coins and the withdrawal of the <a href="/wiki/Penny_(British_pre-decimal_coin)" title="Penny (British pre-decimal coin)">(old) penny</a> (1d) and  <a href="/wiki/Threepence_(British_coin)" title="Threepence (British coin)">(old) threepence</a> (3d) coins.</li>\n<li>1980: Withdrawal of the <a href="/wiki/Sixpence_(British_coin)" title="Sixpence (British coin)">sixpence</a> (6d) coin, which had continued in circulation at a value of <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016"/><span class="frac" role="math">2<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">2</span></span>p.</li>\n<li>1982: The word "new" was dropped from the coinage and a <a href="/wiki/Twenty_pence_(British_decimal_coin)" class="mw-redirect" title="Twenty pence (British decimal coin)">20p coin</a> was introduced.</li>\n<li>1983: A (round, brass) <a href="/wiki/One_pound_(British_decimal_coin)" class="mw-redirect" title="One pound (British decimal coin)">\xc2\xa31 coin</a> was introduced.</li>\n<li>1983: The <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016"/><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">2</span></span>p coin was last produced.</li>\n<li>1984: The <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016"/><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">2</span></span>p coin was withdrawn from circulation.</li>\n<li>1990: The <a href="/wiki/Crown_(British_coin)" title="Crown (British coin)">crown</a>, historically valued at five shillings (25p), was re-tariffed for future issues as a commemorative coin at \xc2\xa35.</li>\n<li>1990: A new <a href="/wiki/Five_pence_(British_decimal_coin)" class="mw-redirect" title="Five pence (British decimal coin)">5p coin</a> was introduced, replacing the original size that had been the same as the <a href="/wiki/Shilling_(British_coin)" title="Shilling (British coin)">shilling coins</a> of the same value that it had in turn replaced. These first generation 5p coins and any remaining old shilling coins were withdrawn from circulation in 1991.</li>\n<li>1992: A new <a href="/wiki/Ten_pence_(British_decimal_coin)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ten pence (British decimal coin)">10p coin</a> was introduced, replacing the original size that had been the same as the <a href="/wiki/Florin_(British_coin)" title="Florin (British coin)">florin or two shilling coins</a> of the same value that it had in turn replaced. These first generation 10p coins and any remaining old florin coins were withdrawn from circulation over the following two years.</li>\n<li>1992: <a href="/wiki/Penny_(British_decimal_coin)" title="Penny (British decimal coin)">1p</a> and <a href="/wiki/Two_pence_(British_decimal_coin)" title="Two pence (British decimal coin)">2p</a> coins began to be minted in <a href="/wiki/Copper_plating" title="Copper plating">copper-plated</a> <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> (the original <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a> coins continued in circulation).</li>\n<li>1997: A new <a href="/wiki/Fifty_pence_(British_decimal_coin)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fifty pence (British decimal coin)">50p</a> coin was introduced, replacing the original size that had been in use since 1969, and  the first generation 50p coins were withdrawn from circulation.</li>\n<li>1998: The <a href="/wiki/Bi-metallic_coins" class="mw-redirect" title="Bi-metallic coins">bi-metallic</a> <a href="/wiki/Two_pounds_(British_decimal_coin)#The_modern_circulating_coin_(1997\xe2\x80\x93present)" class="mw-redirect" title="Two pounds (British decimal coin)">\xc2\xa32 coin</a> was introduced.</li>\n<li>2007: By now the value of copper in the pre-1992 <a href="/wiki/Penny_(British_decimal_coin)" title="Penny (British decimal coin)">1p</a> and <a href="/wiki/Two_pence_(British_decimal_coin)" title="Two pence (British decimal coin)">2p</a> coins (which are 97% copper) exceeded those coins\' face value to such an extent that melting down the coins by <a href="/wiki/Entrepreneur" class="mw-redirect" title="Entrepreneur">entrepreneurs</a> was becoming worthwhile (with a premium of up to 11%, with smelting costs reducing this to around 4%)\xe2\x80\x94although this is illegal, and the <a href="/wiki/Market_value" title="Market value">market value</a> of copper has subsequently fallen dramatically from these earlier peaks.</li>\n<li>In April 2008, an extensive redesign of the coinage was unveiled. The <a href="/wiki/Penny_(British_decimal_coin)" title="Penny (British decimal coin)">1p</a>, <a href="/wiki/Two_pence_(British_decimal_coin)" title="Two pence (British decimal coin)">2p</a>, <a href="/wiki/Five_pence_(British_decimal_coin)" class="mw-redirect" title="Five pence (British decimal coin)">5p</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ten_pence_(British_decimal_coin)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ten pence (British decimal coin)">10p</a>, <a href="/wiki/Twenty_pence_(British_decimal_coin)" class="mw-redirect" title="Twenty pence (British decimal coin)">20p</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fifty_pence_(British_decimal_coin)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fifty pence (British decimal coin)">50p</a> coins feature parts of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_coat_of_arms_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Royal coat of arms of the United Kingdom">Royal Shield</a> on their reverse; and the reverse of the pound coin showed the whole shield. The coins were issued gradually into circulation, starting in mid-2008. They have the same sizes, shapes and weights as those with the old designs which, apart from the round pound coin which was withdrawn in 2017, continue to circulate.</li>\n<li>2012: The <a href="/wiki/Five_pence_(British_decimal_coin)" class="mw-redirect" title="Five pence (British decimal coin)">5p</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ten_pence_(British_decimal_coin)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ten pence (British decimal coin)">10p</a> coins were changed from cupro-nickel to <a href="/wiki/Nickel_electroplating" title="Nickel electroplating">nickel-plated steel</a>.</li>\n<li>2016: The Royal Mint began minting legal tender decimal <a href="/wiki/Sixpence_(British_coin)" title="Sixpence (British coin)">sixpence coins</a> in silver,<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> not intended for regular circulation but to be bought as Christmas presents and for the traditional wedding tradition for the bride: "and a silver sixpence in your shoe".<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>2017: A more secure twelve-sided bi-metallic \xc2\xa31 coin was introduced to reduce forgery. The old round \xc2\xa31 coin ceased to be legal tender on 15 October 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-newpoundcoin_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newpoundcoin-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>As of 2020<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the oldest circulating coins in the UK are the <a href="/wiki/Penny_(British_decimal_coin)" title="Penny (British decimal coin)">1p</a> and <a href="/wiki/Two_pence_(British_decimal_coin)" title="Two pence (British decimal coin)">2p</a> copper coins introduced in 1971. No other coins from before 1982 are in circulation. Prior to the withdrawal from circulation in 1992, the oldest circulating coins had usually dated from 1947: although older coins (shilling; florin, sixpence to 1980) were still legal tender, inflation meant that their silver content was worth more than their face value, which meant that they tended to be removed from circulation. Before decimalisation in 1971, a handful of change might have contained coins 100 or more years old, bearing any of five monarchs\' heads, especially in the copper coins.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Banknotes">Banknotes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Banknotes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Banknotes_of_the_pound_sterling" title="Banknotes of the pound sterling">Banknotes of the pound sterling</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bank_of_England_%C2%A35_reverse.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/bd/Bank_of_England_%C2%A35_reverse.jpg/200px-Bank_of_England_%C2%A35_reverse.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="103" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/bd/Bank_of_England_%C2%A35_reverse.jpg/300px-Bank_of_England_%C2%A35_reverse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/b/bd/Bank_of_England_%C2%A35_reverse.jpg 2x" data-file-width="353" data-file-height="182" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bank_of_England_%C2%A35_reverse.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reverse of a \xc2\xa35 Series G Bank of England note</div></div></div>\n<p>The first sterling notes were issued by the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">Bank of England</a> shortly after its foundation in 1694. Denominations were initially handwritten on the notes at the time of issue. From 1745, the notes were printed in denominations between \xc2\xa320 and \xc2\xa31000, with any odd shillings added by hand. \xc2\xa310 notes were added in 1759, followed by \xc2\xa35 in 1793 and \xc2\xa31 and \xc2\xa32 in 1797. The lowest two denominations were withdrawn after the end of the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_wars" class="mw-redirect" title="Napoleonic wars">Napoleonic wars</a>. In 1855, the notes were converted to being entirely printed, with denominations of \xc2\xa35, \xc2\xa310, \xc2\xa320, \xc2\xa350, \xc2\xa3100, \xc2\xa3200, \xc2\xa3300, \xc2\xa3500 and \xc2\xa31000 issued.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_Scotland" title="Bank of Scotland">Bank of Scotland</a> began issuing notes in 1695. Although the <a href="/wiki/Pound_Scots" title="Pound Scots">pound Scots</a> was still the currency of Scotland, these notes were denominated in sterling in values up to \xc2\xa3100. From 1727, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Bank_of_Scotland" title="Royal Bank of Scotland">Royal Bank of Scotland</a> also issued notes. Both banks issued some notes denominated in guineas as well as pounds. In the 19th century, regulations limited the smallest note issued by Scottish banks to be the \xc2\xa31 denomination, a note not permitted in England.\n</p><p>With the extension of sterling to Ireland in 1825, the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_Ireland" title="Bank of Ireland">Bank of Ireland</a> began issuing sterling notes, later followed by other Irish banks. These notes included the unusual denominations of 30/- and \xc2\xa33. The highest denomination issued by the Irish banks was \xc2\xa3100.\n</p><p>In 1826, banks at least 65 miles (105&#160;km) from London were given permission to issue their own paper money. From 1844, new banks were excluded from issuing notes in England and Wales but not in Scotland and Ireland. Consequently, the number of private banknotes dwindled in England and Wales but proliferated in Scotland and Ireland. The last English private banknotes were issued in 1921.\n</p><p>In 1914, the <a href="/wiki/HM_Treasury" title="HM Treasury">Treasury</a> introduced notes for 10/- and \xc2\xa31 to replace gold coins. These circulated until 1928 when they were replaced by Bank of England notes. Irish independence reduced the number of Irish banks issuing sterling notes to five operating in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>. The <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">Second World War</a> had a drastic effect on the note production of the Bank of England. Fearful of mass forgery by the <a href="/wiki/Nazi" class="mw-redirect" title="Nazi">Nazis</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Operation_Bernhard" title="Operation Bernhard">Operation Bernhard</a>), all notes for \xc2\xa310 and above ceased production, leaving the bank to issue only 10/-, \xc2\xa31 and \xc2\xa35 notes. Scottish and Northern Irish issues were unaffected, with issues in denominations of \xc2\xa31, \xc2\xa35, \xc2\xa310, \xc2\xa320, \xc2\xa350 and \xc2\xa3100.\n</p><p>The Bank of England reintroduced \xc2\xa310 notes in 1964. In 1969, the 10/- note was replaced by the 50p coin to prepare for decimalisation. \xc2\xa320 Bank of England notes were reintroduced in 1970, followed by \xc2\xa350 in 1981.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> A \xc2\xa31 coin was introduced in 1983, and Bank of England \xc2\xa31 notes were withdrawn in 1988. Scottish and Northern Irish banks followed, with only the Royal Bank of Scotland continuing to issue this denomination.\n</p><p>UK notes include raised print (e.g. on the words "Bank of England"); watermarks; embedded metallic thread; holograms; and fluorescent ink visible only under <a href="/wiki/UV_lamp" class="mw-redirect" title="UV lamp">UV lamps</a>. Three printing techniques are involved: <a href="/wiki/Offset_litho" class="mw-redirect" title="Offset litho">offset litho</a>, <a href="/wiki/Intaglio_(printmaking)" title="Intaglio (printmaking)">intaglio</a> and <a href="/wiki/Letterpress" class="mw-redirect" title="Letterpress">letterpress</a>; and the notes incorporate a total of 85 specialized inks.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Bank of England produces notes named "giant" and "titan".<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> A giant is a one million pound note, and a titan is a one hundred million pound bank note,<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> of which there are about 40. Giants and titans are used only within the <a href="/wiki/Banking_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Banking system">banking system</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polymer_banknotes">Polymer banknotes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Polymer banknotes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Northern_Bank_%C2%A35_note" title="Northern Bank \xc2\xa35 note">Northern Bank \xc2\xa35 note</a>, issued by (Northern Ireland\'s) <a href="/wiki/Northern_Bank" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Bank">Northern Bank</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Danske_Bank_(Northern_Ireland)" title="Danske Bank (Northern Ireland)">Danske Bank</a>) in 2000, was the only <a href="/wiki/Polymer_banknote" title="Polymer banknote">polymer banknote</a> in circulation until 2016. The Bank of England introduced \xc2\xa35 polymer banknotes in September 2016, and the paper \xc2\xa35 notes were withdrawn on 5 May 2017. A polymer \xc2\xa310 banknote was introduced on 14 September 2017, and the paper note was withdrawn on 1 March 2018. A polymer \xc2\xa320 banknote was introduced on 20 February 2020, to be followed by a polymer \xc2\xa350 in 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Monetary_policy">Monetary policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Monetary policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>As the <a href="/wiki/Central_bank" title="Central bank">central bank</a> of the United Kingdom which has been delegated authority by the government, the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">Bank of England</a> sets the <a href="/wiki/Monetary_policy" title="Monetary policy">monetary policy</a> for the British pound by controlling the amount of money in circulation.  It has a monopoly on the issuance of banknotes in England and Wales and regulates the amount of banknotes issued by seven authorized banks in Scotland and Northern Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>  <a href="/wiki/HM_Treasury" title="HM Treasury">HM Treasury</a> has reserve powers to give orders to the committee "if they are required in the public interest and by extreme economic circumstances" but such orders must be endorsed by Parliament within 28 days.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Unlike banknotes which have separate issuers in Scotland and Northern Ireland, all UK coins are issued by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mint" title="Royal Mint">Royal Mint</a>, which is an independent enterprise (wholly owned by the Treasury) which also mints coins for other countries.\n</p><p>In Britain\'s <a href="/wiki/Crown_Dependencies" title="Crown Dependencies">Crown Dependencies</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Manx_pound" title="Manx pound">Manx pound</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jersey_pound" title="Jersey pound">Jersey pound</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Guernsey_pound" title="Guernsey pound">Guernsey pound</a> are unregulated by the Bank of England and are issued independently.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>  However, they are maintained at a <a href="/wiki/Fixed_exchange_rate" class="mw-redirect" title="Fixed exchange rate">fixed exchange rate</a> by their respective governments, and Bank of England notes have been made legal tender on the islands, forming a sort of one-way de facto <a href="/wiki/Currency_union" title="Currency union">currency union</a>.  These currencies do not have <a href="/wiki/ISO_4217" title="ISO 4217">ISO 4217</a> codes, so "GBP" is usually used to represent all of them; informal codes are used where the difference is important.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/British_Overseas_Territories" title="British Overseas Territories">British Overseas Territories</a> are responsible for the monetary policy of their own currencies (where they exist),<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> and have their own ISO 4217 codes.  The <a href="/wiki/Falkland_Islands_pound" title="Falkland Islands pound">Falkland Islands pound</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gibraltar_pound" title="Gibraltar pound">Gibraltar pound</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Saint_Helena_pound" title="Saint Helena pound">Saint Helena pound</a> are set at a fixed 1:1 exchange rate with the British pound by local governments.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_tender_and_national_issues">Legal tender and national issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Legal tender and national issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sterling_area.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Sterling_area.svg/220px-Sterling_area.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="325" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Sterling_area.svg/330px-Sterling_area.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Sterling_area.svg/440px-Sterling_area.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="753" data-file-height="1112" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sterling_area.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/British_Islands" title="British Islands">British Islands</a> (red) and overseas territories (blue) using the pound or their local issue</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Legal_tender" title="Legal tender">Legal tender</a> in the United Kingdom is defined such that "a debtor cannot successfully be sued for non-payment if he pays into court in legal tender." Parties can alternatively settle a debt by other means with mutual consent. Strictly speaking, it is necessary for the debtor to offer the exact amount due as there is no obligation for the other party to provide change.<sup id="cite_ref-Mint_LT_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mint_LT-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Throughout the UK, \xc2\xa31 and \xc2\xa32 coins are legal tender for any amount, with the other coins being legal tender only for limited amounts. <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">Bank of England</a> notes are legal tender for any amount in <a href="/wiki/England_and_Wales" title="England and Wales">England and Wales</a>, but not in Scotland or <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mint_LT_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mint_LT-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> (Bank of England 10/- and \xc2\xa31 notes were legal tender, as were Scottish banknotes, during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> under the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Currency_(Defence)_Act_1939&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Currency (Defence) Act 1939 (page does not exist)">Currency (Defence) Act 1939</a>, which was repealed on 1 January 1946.) <a href="/wiki/Channel_Islands" title="Channel Islands">Channel Islands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a> banknotes are legal tender only in their respective jurisdictions.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bank of England, Scottish, Northern Irish, Channel Islands, Isle of Man, Gibraltar, and Falkland banknotes may be offered anywhere in the UK, although there is no obligation to accept them as a means of payment, and acceptance varies. For example, merchants in England generally accept Scottish and Northern Irish bills, but some unfamiliar with them may reject them.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> However, Scottish and Northern Irish bills both tend to be accepted in Scotland and Northern Ireland, respectively. Merchants in England generally do not accept Jersey, Guernsey, Isle of Man, Gibraltar, and Falkland notes but Isle of Man notes are generally accepted in Northern Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Bank of England notes are generally accepted in the Falklands and Gibraltar, but for example, Scottish and Northern Irish notes are not.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Since all of the bills are denominated in pounds sterling, banks will exchange them for locally issued bills at face value,<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (May 2019)">failed verification</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> though some in the UK have had trouble exchanging Falkland Islands pounds.<sup id="cite_ref-Falklands_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Falklands-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Commemorative <a href="/wiki/Five_pounds_(British_decimal_coin)" class="mw-redirect" title="Five pounds (British decimal coin)">\xc2\xa35</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_twenty-five_pence_coin" title="British twenty-five pence coin">25p</a> (crown) coins, and <a href="/wiki/Sixpence_(British_coin)" title="Sixpence (British coin)">6p</a> coins made for traditional wedding ceremonies and Christmas gifts, rarely seen in circulation, are legal tender, as are the <a href="/wiki/Bullion_coin" title="Bullion coin">bullion coins</a> issued by the Mint.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Coin\n</th>\n<th>Maximum usable as legal tender<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>\xc2\xa3100 (produced from 2015)<sup id="cite_ref-Mint_LT_95-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mint_LT-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>unlimited\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>\xc2\xa320 (produced from 2013)\n</td>\n<td>unlimited\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>\xc2\xa35 (post-1990 crown)\n</td>\n<td>unlimited\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>\xc2\xa32\n</td>\n<td>unlimited\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>\xc2\xa31\n</td>\n<td>unlimited\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>50p\n</td>\n<td>\xc2\xa310\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>25p (pre-1990 crown)\n</td>\n<td>\xc2\xa310\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>20p\n</td>\n<td>\xc2\xa310\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>10p\n</td>\n<td>\xc2\xa35\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>5p\n</td>\n<td>\xc2\xa35\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2p\n</td>\n<td>20p\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1p\n</td>\n<td>20p\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Value">Value</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Value">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In 2006, the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_Library" title="House of Commons Library">House of Commons Library</a> published a research paper which included an index of prices in pounds for each year between 1750 and 2005, where 1974 was indexed at 100.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Regarding the period 1750\xe2\x80\x931914 the document states: "Although there was considerable year on year fluctuation in price levels prior to 1914 (reflecting the quality of the harvest, wars, etc.) there was not the long-term steady increase in prices associated with the period since 1945". It goes on to say that "Since 1945 prices have risen in every year with an aggregate rise of over 27 times".\n</p><p>The value of the index in 1751 was 5.1, increasing to a peak of 16.3 in 1813 before declining very soon after the end of the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a> to around 10.0 and remaining in the range 8.5\xe2\x80\x9310.0 at the end of the 19th century. The index was 9.8 in 1914 and peaked at 25.3 in 1920, before declining to 15.8 in 1933 and 1934\xe2\x80\x94prices were only about three times as high as they had been 180 years earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Inflation" title="Inflation">Inflation</a> has had a dramatic effect during and after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>: the index was 20.2 in 1940, 33.0 in 1950, 49.1 in 1960, 73.1 in 1970, 263.7 in 1980, 497.5 in 1990, 671.8 in 2000 and 757.3 in 2005.\n</p><p>The following table shows the equivalent amount of goods and services that, in a particular year, could be purchased with \xc2\xa31.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The table shows that from 1971 to 2015 the British pound lost about 92 per cent of its buying power.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:left">\n<caption>Buying power of one British pound compared to 1971 GBP\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>&#160;Year\n</th>\n<th style="width: 40pt; font-size:95%">&#160;Equivalent&#160; buying power\n</th>\n<th>&#160;Year\n</th>\n<th style="width: 40pt; font-size:95%">&#160;Equivalent&#160; buying power\n</th>\n<th>&#160;Year\n</th>\n<th style="width: 40pt;
